   Family of free and open-source software operating systems based on the
   Linux kernel

   Linux ([65]/ˈlinʊks/ ([66] About this sound [67]listen) [68]LEEN-uuks
   or [69]/ˈlɪnʊks/ [70]LIN-uuks^[71][9]) is a family of [72]open-source
   [73]Unix-like [74]operating systems based on the [75]Linux
   kernel,^[76][10] an [77]operating system kernel first released on
   September 17, 1991, by [78]Linus Torvalds.^[79][11]^[80][12]^[81][13]
   Linux is typically [82]packaged in a [83]Linux distribution.

   Distributions include the Linux kernel and supporting [84]system
   software and [85]libraries, many of which are provided by the [86]GNU
   Project. Many Linux distributions use the word "Linux" in their name,
   but the [87]Free Software Foundation uses the name "GNU/Linux" to
   emphasize the importance of [88]GNU software, [89]causing some
   controversy.^[90][14]^[91][15]

   Popular Linux distributions^[92][16]^[93][17]^[94][18] include
   [95]Debian, [96]Fedora, and [97]Ubuntu. Commercial distributions
   include [98]Red Hat Enterprise Linux and [99]SUSE Linux Enterprise
   Server. Desktop Linux distributions include a [100]windowing system
   such as [101]X11 or [102]Wayland, and a [103]desktop environment such
   as [104]GNOME or [105]KDE Plasma. Distributions intended for
   [106]servers may omit [107]graphics altogether, or include a
   [108]solution stack such as [109]LAMP. Because Linux is freely
   redistributable, anyone may create a distribution for any
   purpose.^[110][19]

   Linux was originally developed for [111]personal computers based on the
   [112]Intel x86 architecture, but has since been [113]ported to more
   [114]platforms than any other operating system.^[115][20] Because of
   the dominance of the Linux-based [116]Android on [117]smartphones,
   Linux also has the [118]largest [119]installed base of all
   [120]general-purpose operating
   systems.^[121][21]^[122][22]^[123][23]^[124][24] Although it is used by
   only around 2.3 percent of [125]desktop computers,^[126][25]^[127][26]
   the [128]Chromebook, which runs the Linux kernel-based [129]Chrome OS,
   dominates the US [130]K–12 education market and represents nearly 20
   percent of sub-$300 [131]notebook sales in the US.^[132][27] Linux is
   the leading operating system on servers (over 96.4% of the top
   1 million web servers' operating systems are Linux),^[133][28] leads
   other [134]big iron systems such as [135]mainframe computers, and is
   the only OS used on [136]TOP500 [137]supercomputers (since November
   2017, having gradually eliminated all
   competitors).^[138][29]^[139][30]^[140][31]

   Linux also runs on [141]embedded systems, i.e. devices whose operating
   system is typically built into the [142]firmware and is highly tailored
   to the system. This includes [143]routers, [144]automation controls,
   [145]smart home technology (like [146]Google Nest),^[147][32]
   [148]televisions (Samsung and LG [149]Smart TVs use [150]Tizen and
   [151]WebOS, respectively),^[152][33]^[153][34]^[154][35]
   [155]automobiles (for example, Tesla, Audi, Mercedes-Benz, Hyundai, and
   Toyota all rely on Linux),^[156][36] [157]digital video recorders,
   [158]video game consoles, and [159]smartwatches.^[160][37] The
   [161]Falcon 9's and the [162]Dragon 2's [163]avionics use a customized
   version of Linux.^[164][38]

   Linux is one of the most prominent examples of free and open-source
   [165]software collaboration. The [166]source code may be used, modified
   and distributed commercially or non-commercially by anyone under the
   terms of its respective licenses, such as the [167]GNU General Public
   License.^[168][19]

   90% of all cloud infrastructure is powered by Linux including
   supercomputers and cloud providers.^[169][39] 74% of smartphones in the
   world are Linux-based.^[170][40]
   [ ]

History[[206]edit]

   Main article: [207]History of Linux

Precursors[[208]edit]

   [209]Linus Torvalds, principal author of the [210]Linux kernel

   The [211]Unix operating system was conceived and implemented in 1969,
   at [212]AT&T's [213]Bell Labs, in the United States by [214]Ken
   Thompson, [215]Dennis Ritchie, [216]Douglas McIlroy, and [217]Joe
   Ossanna.^[218][41] First released in 1971, Unix was written entirely in
   [219]assembly language, as was common practice at the time. In 1973 in
   a key, pioneering approach, it was rewritten in the [220]C programming
   language by [221]Dennis Ritchie (with the exception of some hardware
   and I/O routines). The availability of a [222]high-level language
   implementation of Unix made its [223]porting to different computer
   platforms easier.^[224][42]

   Due to an earlier [225]antitrust case forbidding it from entering the
   computer business, AT&T was required to license the operating system's
   source code to anyone who asked. As a result, Unix grew quickly and
   became widely adopted by academic institutions and businesses. In 1984,
   AT&T divested itself of Bell Labs; freed of the legal obligation
   requiring free licensing, Bell Labs began selling Unix as a
   [226]proprietary product, where users were not legally allowed to
   modify Unix. The [227]GNU Project, started in 1983 by [228]Richard
   Stallman, had the goal of creating a "complete Unix-compatible software
   system" composed entirely of [229]free software. Work began in
   1984.^[230][43] Later, in 1985, Stallman started the [231]Free Software
   Foundation and wrote the [232]GNU General Public License (GNU GPL) in
   1989. By the early 1990s, many of the programs required in an operating
   system (such as libraries, [233]compilers, [234]text editors, a
   [235]command-line shell, and a [236]windowing system) were completed,
   although low-level elements such as [237]device drivers, [238]daemons,
   and the [239]kernel, called [240]GNU Hurd, were stalled and
   incomplete.^[241][44]

   Linus Torvalds has stated that if the [242]GNU kernel had been
   available at the time (1991), he would not have decided to write his
   own.^[243][45] Although not released until 1992, due to [244]legal
   complications, development of [245]386BSD, from which [246]NetBSD,
   [247]OpenBSD and [248]FreeBSD descended, predated that of Linux.
   Torvalds has also stated that if 386BSD had been available at the time,
   he probably would not have created Linux.^[249][46]

   [250]MINIX was created by [251]Andrew S. Tanenbaum, a [252]computer
   science professor, and released in 1987 as a minimal [253]Unix-like
   operating system targeted at students and others who wanted to learn
   operating system principles. Although the complete source code of MINIX
   was freely available, the licensing terms prevented it from being
   [254]free software until the licensing changed in April 2000.^[255][47]

Creation[[256]edit]

   In 1991, while attending the [257]University of Helsinki, Torvalds
   became curious about operating systems.^[258][48] Frustrated by the
   licensing of MINIX, which at the time limited it to educational use
   only,^[259][47] he began to work on his own operating system kernel,
   which eventually became the [260]Linux kernel.

   Torvalds began the development of the Linux kernel on MINIX and
   applications written for MINIX were also used on Linux. Later, Linux
   matured and further Linux kernel development took place on Linux
   systems.^[261][49] GNU applications also replaced all MINIX components,
   because it was advantageous to use the freely available code from the
   GNU Project with the fledgling operating system; code licensed under
   the GNU GPL can be reused in other computer programs as long as they
   also are released under the same or a compatible license. Torvalds
   initiated a switch from his original license, which prohibited
   commercial redistribution, to the GNU GPL.^[262][50] Developers worked
   to integrate GNU components with the Linux kernel, making a fully
   functional and free operating system.^[263][51]

Naming[[264]edit]

   5.25-inch [265]floppy disks holding a very early version of Linux

   Linus Torvalds had wanted to call his invention "Freax", a
   [266]portmanteau of "free", "freak", and "x" (as an allusion to Unix).
   During the start of his work on the system, some of the project's
   [267]makefiles included the name "Freax" for about half a year.
   Torvalds had already considered the name "Linux", but initially
   dismissed it as too egotistical.^[268][52]

   In order to facilitate development, the files were uploaded to the
   [269]FTP server (ftp.funet.fi) of [270]FUNET in September 1991. Ari
   Lemmke, Torvalds' coworker at the [271]Helsinki University of
   Technology (HUT), who was one of the volunteer administrators for the
   FTP server at the time, did not think that "Freax" was a good name, so
   he named the project "Linux" on the server without consulting
   Torvalds.^[272][52] Later, however, Torvalds consented to "Linux".

   According to a newsgroup post by Torvalds,^[273][9] the word "Linux"
   should be pronounced ([274]/ˈlɪnʊks/ ([275] About this sound
   [276]listen) [277]LIN-uuks) with a short ‘i’ as in ‘print’ and ‘u’ as
   in ‘put’. To further demonstrate how the word "Linux" should be
   pronounced, he included an audio guide ([278] About this sound
   [279]listen ([280]help·[281]info)) with the kernel source
   code.^[282][53] Contradictory, in this recording, he pronounces ‘Linux’
   ([283]/ˈlinʊks/ ([284] About this sound [285]listen) [286]LEEN-uuks
   with a short but close unrounded front vowel.

Commercial and popular uptake[[287]edit]

   Main article: [288]Linux adoption
   [289]Ubuntu, a popular Linux distribution
   [290]Nexus 5X running [291]Android

   Adoption of Linux in production environments, rather than being used
   only by hobbyists, started to take off first in the mid-1990s in the
   supercomputing community, where organizations such as [292]NASA started
   to replace their increasingly expensive machines with [293]clusters of
   inexpensive commodity computers running Linux. Commercial use began
   when [294]Dell and [295]IBM, followed by [296]Hewlett-Packard, started
   offering Linux support to escape [297]Microsoft's monopoly in the
   desktop operating system market.^[298][54]

   Today, Linux systems are used throughout computing, from [299]embedded
   systems to virtually all [300]supercomputers,^[301][31]^[302][55] and
   have secured a place in [303]server installations such as the popular
   [304]LAMP application stack. Use of Linux distributions in home and
   enterprise desktops has been
   growing.^[305][56]^[306][57]^[307][58]^[308][59]^[309][60]^[310][61]^[3
   11][62] Linux distributions have also become popular in the
   [312]netbook market, with many devices shipping with customized Linux
   distributions installed, and Google releasing their own [313]Chrome OS
   designed for netbooks.

   Linux's greatest success in the consumer market is perhaps the mobile
   device market, with [314]Android being one of the most dominant
   operating systems on [315]smartphones and very popular on [316]tablets
   and, more recently, on [317]wearables. Linux gaming is also on the rise
   with [318]Valve showing its support for Linux and rolling out
   [319]SteamOS, its own gaming-oriented Linux distribution. Linux
   distributions have also gained popularity with various local and
   national governments, such as the federal government of
   [320]Brazil.^[321][63]

Current development[[322]edit]

   [323]In-flight entertainment system booting up displaying the Linux
   logo

   [324]Greg Kroah-Hartman is the lead maintainer for the Linux kernel and
   guides its development.^[325][64] [326]William John Sullivan is the
   executive director of the Free Software Foundation,^[327][65] which in
   turn supports the GNU components.^[328][66] Finally, individuals and
   corporations develop third-party non-GNU components. These third-party
   components comprise a vast body of work and may include both kernel
   modules and user applications and libraries.

   Linux vendors and communities combine and distribute the kernel, GNU
   components, and non-GNU components, with additional [329]package
   management software in the form of [330]Linux distributions.

Design[[331]edit]

   Many [332]open source developers agree that the Linux kernel was not
   designed but rather [333]evolved through [334]natural selection.
   Torvalds considers that although the design of Unix served as a
   scaffolding, "Linux grew with a lot of mutations - and because the
   mutations were less than random, they were faster and more directed
   than [335]alpha-particles in DNA." ^[336][67] Raymond considers Linux's
   revolutionary aspects to be social, not technical: before Linux complex
   software was designed carefully by small groups, but "Linux evolved in
   a completely different way. From nearly the beginning, it was rather
   casually hacked on by huge numbers of volunteers coordinating only
   through the Internet. Quality was maintained not by rigid standards or
   autocracy but by the naively simple strategy of releasing every week
   and getting feedback from hundreds of users within days, creating a
   sort of rapid Darwinian selection on the mutations introduced by
   developers."^[337][68] Bryan Cantrill, an engineer of a competing OS,
   agrees that "Linux wasn't designed, it evolved", but considers this to
   be a limitation, proposing that some features, especially those related
   to security,^[338][69] cannot be evolved into, "this is not a
   biological system at the end of the day, it's a software system."
   ^[339][70] A Linux-based system is a modular [340]Unix-like operating
   system, deriving much of its basic design from principles established
   in Unix during the 1970s and 1980s. Such a system uses a
   [341]monolithic kernel, the [342]Linux kernel, which handles process
   control, networking, access to the [343]peripherals, and [344]file
   systems. [345]Device drivers are either integrated directly with the
   kernel, or added as modules that are loaded while the system is
   running.^[346][71]

   The GNU [347]userland is a key part of most systems based on the Linux
   kernel, with Android being the notable exception. The Project's
   [348]implementation of the [349]C library works as a wrapper for the
   system calls of the Linux kernel necessary to the kernel-userspace
   interface, the [350]toolchain is a broad collection of programming
   tools vital to Linux development (including the [351]compilers used to
   build the Linux kernel itself), and the [352]coreutils implement many
   basic [353]Unix tools. The project also develops [354]Bash, a popular
   [355]CLI [356]shell. The [357]graphical user interface (or GUI) used by
   most Linux systems is built on top of an implementation of the [358]X
   Window System.^[359][72] More recently, the Linux community seeks to
   advance to [360]Wayland as the new display server protocol in place of
   [361]X11. Many other open-source software projects contribute to Linux
   systems.

   CAPTION: Various layers within Linux, also showing separation between
   the [362]userland and [363]kernel space

   User mode User applications [364]bash, [365]LibreOffice, [366]GIMP,
   [367]Blender, [368]0 A.D., [369]Mozilla Firefox, ...
   System components [370]Daemons:
   [371]systemd, [372]runit, logind, networkd, [373]PulseAudio, ...
   [374]Window manager:
   [375]X11, [376]Wayland, [377]SurfaceFlinger (Android) Graphics:
   [378]Mesa, [379]AMD Catalyst, ... Other libraries:
   [380]GTK+, [381]Qt, [382]EFL, [383]SDL, [384]SFML, [385]FLTK,
   [386]GNUstep, ...
   [387]C standard library open(), exec(), sbrk(), socket(), fopen(),
   calloc(), ... (up to 2000 [388]subroutines)
   [389]glibc aims to be fast, [390]musl and [391]uClibc target embedded
   systems, [392]bionic written for [393]Android, etc. All aim to be
   [394]POSIX/[395]SUS-compatible.
   Kernel mode [396]Linux kernel [397]stat, [398]splice, [399]dup,
   [400]read, [401]open, [402]ioctl, [403]write, [404]mmap, [405]close,
   [406]exit, etc. (about 380 system calls)
   The Linux kernel [407]System Call Interface (SCI, aims to be
   [408]POSIX/[409]SUS-compatible)^[[410]citation needed]
   [411]Process scheduling
   subsystem [412]IPC
   subsystem [413]Memory management
   subsystem [414]Virtual files
   subsystem Network
   subsystem
   Other components: [415]ALSA, [416]DRI, [417]evdev, [418]LVM,
   [419]device mapper, [420]Linux Network Scheduler, [421]Netfilter
   [422]Linux Security Modules: [423]SELinux, [424]TOMOYO, [425]AppArmor,
   [426]Smack
   Hardware ([427]CPU, [428]main memory, [429]data storage devices, etc.)

   Installed components of a Linux system include the
   following:^[430][72]^[431][73]
     * A [432]bootloader, for example [433]GNU GRUB, [434]LILO,
       [435]SYSLINUX, or [436]Gummiboot. This is a program that loads the
       Linux kernel into the computer's [437]main memory, by being
       executed by the computer when it is turned on and after the
       [438]firmware initialization is performed.
     * An [439]init program, such as the traditional [440]sysvinit and the
       newer [441]systemd, [442]OpenRC and [443]Upstart. This is the first
       [444]process launched by the Linux kernel, and is at the root of
       the process tree: in other terms, all processes are launched
       through init. It starts processes such as system services and login
       prompts (whether graphical or in terminal mode).
     * [445]Software libraries, which contain code that can be used by
       running processes. On Linux systems using [446]ELF-format
       executable files, the [447]dynamic linker that manages use of
       dynamic libraries is known as [448]ld-linux.so. If the system is
       set up for the user to compile software themselves, [449]header
       files will also be included to describe the [450]interface of
       installed libraries. Besides the most commonly used software
       library on Linux systems, the [451]GNU C Library (glibc), there are
       numerous other libraries, such as [452]SDL and [453]Mesa.
          + [454]C standard library is the library needed to run [455]C
            programs on a computer system, with the GNU C Library being
            the standard. For embedded systems, alternatives such as the
            [456]musl, [457]EGLIBC (a glibc fork once used by Debian) and
            [458]uClibc (which was designed for [459]uClinux) have been
            developed, although the last two are no longer maintained.
            Android uses its own C library, [460]Bionic.
     * Basic Unix commands, with GNU coreutils being the standard
       implementation. Alternatives exist for embedded systems, such as
       the copyleft [461]BusyBox, and the BSD-licensed [462]Toybox.
     * [463]Widget toolkits are the libraries used to build [464]graphical
       user interfaces (GUIs) for software applications. Numerous widget
       toolkits are available, including [465]GTK and [466]Clutter
       developed by the [467]GNOME project, [468]Qt developed by the
       [469]Qt Project and led by [470]Digia, and [471]Enlightenment
       Foundation Libraries (EFL) developed primarily by the
       [472]Enlightenment team.
     * A [473]package management system, such as [474]dpkg and [475]RPM.
       Alternatively packages can be compiled from binary or source
       [476]tarballs.
     * User interface programs such as command shells or windowing
       environments.

User interface[[477]edit]

   The [478]user interface, also known as the [479]shell, is either a
   [480]command-line interface (CLI), a [481]graphical user interface
   (GUI), or controls attached to the associated hardware, which is common
   for [482]embedded systems. For desktop systems, the default user
   interface is usually graphical, although the CLI is commonly available
   through [483]terminal emulator windows or on a separate [484]virtual
   console.

   CLI shells are text-based user interfaces, which use text for both
   input and output. The dominant shell used in Linux is the
   [485]Bourne-Again Shell (bash), originally developed for the [486]GNU
   project. Most low-level Linux components, including various parts of
   the [487]userland, use the CLI exclusively. The CLI is particularly
   suited for automation of repetitive or delayed tasks and provides very
   simple [488]inter-process communication.

   On desktop systems, the most popular user interfaces are the [489]GUI
   shells, packaged together with extensive [490]desktop environments,
   such as [491]KDE Plasma, [492]GNOME, [493]MATE, [494]Cinnamon,
   [495]LXDE, [496]Pantheon and [497]Xfce, though a variety of additional
   user interfaces exist. Most popular user interfaces are based on the
   [498]X Window System, often simply called "X". It provides [499]network
   transparency and permits a graphical application running on one system
   to be displayed on another where a user may interact with the
   application; however, certain extensions of the X Window System are not
   capable of working over the network.^[500][74] Several X display
   servers exist, with the reference implementation, [501]X.Org Server,
   being the most popular.

   [502]Server distributions might provide a command-line interface for
   developers and administrators, but provide a custom interface towards
   end-users, designed for the use-case of the system. This custom
   interface is accessed through a client that resides on another system,
   not necessarily Linux based.

   Several types of [503]window managers exist for X11, including
   [504]tiling, [505]dynamic, [506]stacking and [507]compositing. Window
   managers provide means to control the placement and appearance of
   individual application windows, and interact with the X Window System.
   Simpler [508]X window managers such as [509]dwm, [510]ratpoison,
   [511]i3wm, or herbstluftwm provide a [512]minimalist functionality,
   while more elaborate window managers such as [513]FVWM,
   [514]Enlightenment or [515]Window Maker provide more features such as a
   built-in [516]taskbar and [517]themes, but are still lightweight when
   compared to desktop environments. Desktop environments include window
   managers as part of their standard installations, such as [518]Mutter
   (GNOME), [519]KWin (KDE) or [520]Xfwm (xfce), although users may choose
   to use a different window manager if preferred.

   [521]Wayland is a display server protocol intended as a replacement for
   the X11 protocol; as of 2014^[522][update], it has not received wider
   adoption. Unlike X11, Wayland does not need an external window manager
   and compositing manager. Therefore, a Wayland compositor takes the role
   of the display server, window manager and compositing manager. Weston
   is the reference implementation of Wayland, while GNOME's Mutter and
   KDE's KWin are being ported to Wayland as standalone display servers.
   Enlightenment has already been successfully ported since version
   19.^[523][75]

Video input infrastructure[[524]edit]

   Main article: [525]Video4Linux

   Linux currently has two modern kernel-userspace APIs for handling video
   input devices: [526]V4L2 API for video streams and radio, and [527]DVB
   API for digital TV reception.^[528][76]

   Due to the complexity and diversity of different devices, and due to
   the large number of formats and standards handled by those APIs, this
   infrastructure needs to evolve to better fit other devices. Also, a
   good userspace device library is the key of the success for having
   userspace applications to be able to work with all formats supported by
   those devices.^[529][77]^[530][78]

Development[[531]edit]

   Simplified history of [532]Unix-like operating systems. Linux shares
   similar architecture and concepts (as part of the [533]POSIX standard)
   but does not share non-free source code with the original [534]Unix or
   [535]MINIX.
   Main articles: [536]Linux distribution and [537]Free software

   The primary difference between Linux and many other popular
   contemporary operating systems is that the [538]Linux kernel and other
   components are [539]free and [540]open-source software. Linux is not
   the only such operating system, although it is by far the most widely
   used.^[541][79] Some [542]free and [543]open-source software licenses
   are based on the principle of [544]copyleft, a kind of reciprocity: any
   work derived from a copyleft piece of software must also be copyleft
   itself. The most common free software license, the [545]GNU General
   Public License (GPL), is a form of copyleft, and is used for the Linux
   kernel and many of the components from the [546]GNU Project.^[547][80]

   Linux-based distributions are intended by developers for
   [548]interoperability with other operating systems and established
   computing standards. Linux systems adhere to [549]POSIX,^[550][81]
   [551]SUS,^[552][82] [553]LSB, [554]ISO, and [555]ANSI standards where
   possible, although to date only one Linux distribution has been POSIX.1
   certified, Linux-FT.^[556][83]^[557][84]

   Free software projects, although developed through [558]collaboration,
   are often produced independently of each other. The fact that the
   software licenses explicitly permit redistribution, however, provides a
   basis for larger-scale projects that collect the software produced by
   stand-alone projects and make it available all at once in the form of a
   Linux distribution.

   Many Linux distributions manage a remote collection of system software
   and application software packages available for download and
   installation through a network connection. This allows users to adapt
   the operating system to their specific needs. Distributions are
   maintained by individuals, loose-knit teams, volunteer organizations,
   and commercial entities. A distribution is responsible for the default
   configuration of the installed Linux kernel, general system security,
   and more generally integration of the different software packages into
   a coherent whole. Distributions typically use a [559]package manager
   such as [560]apt, [561]yum, [562]zypper, [563]pacman or [564]portage to
   install, remove, and update all of a system's software from one central
   location.^[565][85]

Community[[566]edit]

   See also: [567]Free software community and [568]Linux User Group

   A distribution is largely driven by its developer and user communities.
   Some vendors develop and fund their distributions on a volunteer basis,
   [569]Debian being a well-known example. Others maintain a community
   version of their commercial distributions, as [570]Red Hat does with
   [571]Fedora, and [572]SUSE does with [573]openSUSE.^[574][86]^[575][87]

   In many cities and regions, local associations known as [576]Linux User
   Groups (LUGs) seek to promote their preferred distribution and by
   extension free software. They hold meetings and provide free
   demonstrations, training, technical support, and operating system
   installation to new users. Many Internet communities also provide
   support to Linux users and developers. Most distributions and free
   software / open-source projects have [577]IRC chatrooms or
   [578]newsgroups. [579]Online forums are another means for support, with
   notable examples being [580]LinuxQuestions.org and the various
   distribution specific support and community forums, such as ones for
   [581]Ubuntu, [582]Fedora, and [583]Gentoo. Linux distributions host
   [584]mailing lists; commonly there will be a specific topic such as
   usage or development for a given list.

   There are several technology websites with a Linux focus. Print
   magazines on Linux often bundle [585]cover disks that carry software or
   even complete Linux distributions.^[586][88]^[587][89]

   Although Linux distributions are generally available without charge,
   several large corporations sell, support, and contribute to the
   development of the components of the system and of [588]free software.
   An analysis of the Linux kernel showed 75 percent of the code from
   December 2008 to January 2010 was developed by programmers working for
   corporations, leaving about 18 percent to volunteers and 7%
   unclassified.^[589][90] Major corporations that provide contributions
   include [590]Dell, [591]IBM, [592]HP, [593]Oracle, [594]Sun
   Microsystems (now part of Oracle) and [595]Nokia. A number of
   corporations, notably Red Hat, [596]Canonical and [597]SUSE, have built
   a significant business around Linux distributions.

   The [598]free software licenses, on which the various software packages
   of a distribution built on the Linux kernel are based, explicitly
   accommodate and encourage commercialization; the relationship between a
   Linux distribution as a whole and individual vendors may be seen as
   [599]symbiotic. One common [600]business model of commercial suppliers
   is charging for support, especially for business users. A number of
   companies also offer a specialized business version of their
   distribution, which adds proprietary support packages and tools to
   administer higher numbers of installations or to simplify
   administrative tasks.

   Another business model is to give away the software in order to sell
   hardware. This used to be the norm in the computer industry, with
   operating systems such as [601]CP/M, [602]Apple DOS and versions of
   [603]Mac OS prior to 7.6 freely copyable (but not modifiable). As
   computer hardware standardized throughout the 1980s, it became more
   difficult for hardware manufacturers to profit from this tactic, as the
   OS would run on any manufacturer's computer that shared the same
   architecture.

Programming on Linux[[604]edit]

   Most [605]programming languages support Linux either directly or
   through third-party community based [606]ports.^[607][91] The original
   development tools used for building both Linux applications and
   operating system programs are found within the [608]GNU toolchain,
   which includes the [609]GNU Compiler Collection (GCC) and the [610]GNU
   Build System. Amongst others, GCC provides compilers for [611]Ada,
   [612]C, [613]C++, [614]Go and [615]Fortran. Many programming languages
   have a cross-platform reference implementation that supports Linux, for
   example [616]PHP, [617]Perl, [618]Ruby, [619]Python, [620]Java, Go,
   [621]Rust and [622]Haskell. First released in 2003, the [623]LLVM
   project provides an alternative cross-platform open-source compiler for
   many languages. [624]Proprietary compilers for Linux include the
   [625]Intel C++ Compiler, [626]Sun Studio, and IBM XL [627]C/[628]C++
   Compiler. [629]BASIC in the form of [630]Visual Basic is supported in
   such forms as [631]Gambas, [632]FreeBASIC, and [633]XBasic, and in
   terms of terminal programming or [634]QuickBASIC or [635]Turbo BASIC
   programming in the form of [636]QB64.

   A common feature of Unix-like systems, Linux includes traditional
   specific-purpose programming languages targeted at [637]scripting, text
   processing and system configuration and management in general. Linux
   distributions support [638]shell scripts, [639]awk, [640]sed and
   [641]make. Many programs also have an embedded programming language to
   support configuring or programming themselves. For example,
   [642]regular expressions are supported in programs like [643]grep and
   [644]locate, the traditional Unix [645]MTA [646]Sendmail contains its
   own [647]Turing complete scripting system, and the advanced text editor
   [648]GNU Emacs is built around a general purpose [649]Lisp interpreter.

   Most distributions also include support for [650]PHP, [651]Perl,
   [652]Ruby, [653]Python and other [654]dynamic languages. While not as
   common, Linux also supports [655]C# (via [656]Mono), [657]Vala, and
   [658]Scheme. [659]Guile Scheme acts as an [660]extension language
   targeting the [661]GNU system utilities, seeking to make the
   conventionally small, [662]static, [663]compiled [664]C programs of
   [665]Unix design rapidly and dynamically extensible via an elegant,
   [666]functional high-level scripting system; many GNU programs can be
   compiled with optional Guile [667]bindings to this end. A number of
   [668]Java Virtual Machines and development kits run on Linux, including
   the original Sun Microsystems JVM ([669]HotSpot), and IBM's J2SE RE, as
   well as many open-source projects like [670]Kaffe and [671]JikesRVM.

   [672]GNOME and [673]KDE are popular [674]desktop environments and
   provide a framework for developing applications. These projects are
   based on the [675]GTK and [676]Qt [677]widget toolkits, respectively,
   which can also be used independently of the larger framework. Both
   support a wide variety of languages. There are a number of
   [678]Integrated development environments available including
   [679]Anjuta, [680]Code::Blocks, [681]CodeLite, [682]Eclipse,
   [683]Geany, [684]ActiveState Komodo, [685]KDevelop, [686]Lazarus,
   [687]MonoDevelop, [688]NetBeans, and [689]Qt Creator, while the
   long-established editors [690]Vim, [691]nano and [692]Emacs remain
   popular.^[693][92]

Hardware support[[694]edit]

   Linux is ubiquitously found on various types of hardware.
   See also: [695]List of Linux-supported computer architectures

   The Linux kernel is a widely [696]ported operating system kernel,
   available for devices ranging from mobile phones to supercomputers; it
   runs on a highly diverse range of [697]computer architectures,
   including the hand-held [698]ARM-based [699]iPAQ and the [700]IBM
   [701]mainframes [702]System z9 or [703]System z10.^[704][93]
   Specialized distributions and kernel forks exist for less mainstream
   architectures; for example, the [705]ELKS kernel [706]fork can run on
   [707]Intel 8086 or [708]Intel 80286 [709]16-bit microprocessors, while
   the [710]µClinux kernel fork may run on systems without a [711]memory
   management unit. The kernel also runs on architectures that were only
   ever intended to use a manufacturer-created operating system, such as
   [712]Macintosh computers^[[713]citation needed] (with both [714]PowerPC
   and [715]Intel processors), [716]PDAs, [717]video game consoles,
   [718]portable music players, and mobile phones.

   There are several industry associations and hardware [719]conferences
   devoted to maintaining and improving support for diverse hardware under
   Linux, such as [720]FreedomHEC. Over time, support for different
   hardware has improved in Linux, resulting in any off-the-shelf purchase
   having a "good chance" of being compatible.^[721][94]

   In 2014, a new initiative was launched to automatically collect a
   database of all tested hardware configurations.^[722][95]

Uses[[723]edit]

   See also: [724]Linux range of use

   Besides the Linux distributions designed for general-purpose use on
   desktops and servers, distributions may be specialized for different
   purposes including: [725]computer architecture support, [726]embedded
   systems, stability, security, localization to a specific region or
   language, targeting of specific user groups, support for [727]real-time
   applications, or commitment to a given desktop environment.
   Furthermore, some distributions deliberately include only [728]free
   software. As of 2015^[729][update], over four hundred Linux
   distributions are actively developed, with about a dozen distributions
   being most popular for general-purpose use.^[730][96]

Desktop[[731]edit]

   Visible software components of the Linux desktop stack include the
   [732]display server, [733]widget engines, and some of the more
   widespread [734]widget toolkits. There are also components not directly
   visible to end-users, including [735]D-Bus and [736]PulseAudio.
   See also: [737]Desktop environment, [738]Linux adoption: Measuring
   desktop adoption, and [739]Criticism of desktop Linux

   The popularity of Linux on standard desktop computers and laptops has
   been increasing over the years.^[740][97] Most modern distributions
   include a graphical user environment, with, as of
   February 2015^[741][update], the two most popular environments being
   the [742]KDE Plasma Desktop and
   [743]Xfce.^[744][98]^[[745]better source needed]

   No single official Linux desktop exists: rather desktop environments
   and Linux distributions select components from a pool of [746]free and
   open-source software with which they construct a GUI implementing some
   more or less strict design guide. GNOME, for example, has its
   [747]human interface guidelines as a design guide, which gives the
   [748]human–machine interface an important role, not just when doing the
   graphical design, but also when considering people with
   [749]disabilities, and even when focusing on security.^[750][99]

   The collaborative nature of free software development allows
   distributed teams to perform [751]language localization of some Linux
   distributions for use in locales where localizing proprietary systems
   would not be cost-effective. For example, the [752]Sinhalese language
   version of the [753]Knoppix distribution became available significantly
   before Microsoft translated [754]Windows XP into Sinhalese.^[755][100]
   In this case the [756]Lanka Linux User Group played a major part in
   developing the localized system by combining the knowledge of
   university professors, [757]linguists, and local developers.

Performance and applications[[758]edit]

   The performance of Linux on the desktop has been a controversial
   topic;^[759][101] for example in 2007 [760]Con Kolivas accused the
   Linux community of favoring performance on servers. He quit Linux
   kernel development out of frustration with this lack of focus on the
   desktop, and then gave a "tell all" interview on the topic.^[761][102]
   Since then a significant amount of development has focused on improving
   the desktop experience. Projects such as [762]systemd and [763]Upstart
   (deprecated in 2014) aim for a faster boot time; the Wayland and Mir
   projects aim at replacing [764]X11 while enhancing desktop performance,
   security and appearance.^[765][103]

   Many popular applications are available for a wide variety of operating
   systems. For example, [766]Mozilla Firefox,
   [767]OpenOffice.org/[768]LibreOffice and [769]Blender have downloadable
   versions for all major operating systems. Furthermore, some
   applications initially developed for Linux, such as [770]Pidgin, and
   [771]GIMP, were ported to other operating systems (including
   [772]Windows and [773]macOS) due to their popularity. In addition, a
   growing number of proprietary desktop applications are also supported
   on Linux,^[774][104] such as [775]Autodesk Maya and [776]The Foundry's
   Nuke in the high-end field of animation and visual effects; see the
   [777]list of proprietary software for Linux for more details. There are
   also [778]several companies that have ported their own or other
   companies' games to Linux, with Linux also being a supported platform
   on both the popular [779]Steam and [780]Desura digital-distribution
   services.^[781][105]

   Many other types of applications available for Microsoft Windows and
   macOS also run on Linux. Commonly, either a [782]free software
   application will exist which does the functions of an application found
   on another operating system, or that application will have a version
   that works on Linux, such as with [783]Skype and some [784]video games
   like [785]Dota 2 and [786]Team Fortress 2. Furthermore, the [787]Wine
   project provides a Windows compatibility layer to run unmodified
   Windows applications on Linux. It is sponsored by commercial interests
   including [788]CodeWeavers, which produces a commercial version of the
   software. Since 2009, Google has also provided funding to the Wine
   project.^[789][106]^[790][107] [791]CrossOver, a proprietary solution
   based on the open-source Wine project, supports running Windows
   versions of [792]Microsoft Office, [793]Intuit applications such as
   [794]Quicken and [795]QuickBooks, [796]Adobe Photoshop versions through
   CS2, and many popular games such as [797]World of Warcraft. In other
   cases, where there is no Linux port of some software in areas such as
   [798]desktop publishing^[799][108] and [800]professional
   audio,^[801][109]^[802][110]^[803][111] there is equivalent software
   available on Linux. It is also possible to run applications written for
   [804]Android on other versions of Linux using [805]Anbox.

Components and installation[[806]edit]

   Besides externally visible components, such as [807]X window managers,
   a non-obvious but quite central role is played by the programs hosted
   by [808]freedesktop.org, such as [809]D-Bus or [810]PulseAudio; both
   major desktop environments (GNOME and KDE) include them, each offering
   graphical front-ends written using the corresponding toolkit ([811]GTK
   or [812]Qt). A [813]display server is another component, which for the
   longest time has been communicating in the X11 display server protocol
   with its clients; prominent software talking X11 includes the
   [814]X.Org Server and [815]Xlib. Frustration over the cumbersome X11
   core protocol, and especially over its numerous extensions, has led to
   the creation of a new display server protocol, [816]Wayland.

   Installing, updating and removing software in Linux is typically done
   through the use of package managers such as the [817]Synaptic Package
   Manager, [818]PackageKit, and [819]Yum Extender. While most major Linux
   distributions have extensive repositories, often containing tens of
   thousands of packages, not all the software that can run on Linux is
   available from the official repositories. Alternatively, users can
   install packages from unofficial repositories, download pre-compiled
   packages directly from websites, or compile the source code by
   themselves. All these methods come with different degrees of
   difficulty; compiling the source code is in general considered a
   challenging process for new Linux users, but it is hardly needed in
   modern distributions and is not a method specific to Linux.
     * Samples of graphical desktop interfaces
     * [820]GNOME
     * [821]KDE Plasma 5
     * [822]Cinnamon
     * [823]Mate
     * [824]Pantheon
     * [825]Budgie
     * [826]Xfce
     * [827]Unity (discontinued; forked by UBPorts for Unity 8 (Now
       Lomiri) desktop; still used in various remixes and forks of Ubuntu)
     * [828]LXDE
     * [829]LXQt
     * [830]i3-gaps
     * [831]Enlightenment
     * [832]Fluxbox
     * [833]Sugar
     * [834]Trinity

Netbooks[[835]edit]

   Linux distributions have also become popular in the [836]netbook
   market, with many devices such as the [837]Asus Eee PC and [838]Acer
   Aspire One shipping with customized Linux distributions
   installed.^[839][112]

   In 2009, Google announced its [840]Chrome OS as a minimal Linux-based
   operating system, using the [841]Chrome browser as the main user
   interface. Chrome OS initially did not run any non-web applications,
   except for the bundled file manager and media player. A certain level
   of support for [842]Android applications was added in later
   versions.^[843][113] As of 2018, Google added the ability to install
   any Linux software in a container,^[844][114] enabling Chrome OS to be
   used like any other Linux distribution. Netbooks that shipped with the
   operating system, termed [845]Chromebooks, started appearing on the
   market in June 2011.^[846][115]

Servers, mainframes and supercomputers[[847]edit]

   Broad overview of the [848]LAMP software bundle, displayed here
   together with [849]Squid. A high-performance and high-availability web
   server solution providing security in a hostile environment.

   [850]Linux distributions have long been used as [851]server operating
   systems, and have risen to prominence in that area; [852]Netcraft
   reported in September 2006, that eight of the ten (other two with
   "unknown" OS) most reliable internet hosting companies ran Linux
   distributions on their [853]web servers,^[854][116] with Linux in the
   top position. In June 2008, Linux distributions represented five of the
   top ten, [855]FreeBSD three of ten, and [856]Microsoft two of
   ten;^[857][117] since February 2010, Linux distributions represented
   six of the top ten, FreeBSD three of ten, and Microsoft one of
   ten,^[858][118] with Linux in the top position.

   Linux distributions are the cornerstone of the [859]LAMP
   server-software combination (Linux, [860]Apache,
   [861]MariaDB/[862]MySQL, [863]Perl/[864]PHP/[865]Python) which has
   achieved popularity among developers, and which is one of the more
   common platforms for website hosting.^[866][119]

   Linux distributions have become increasingly popular on
   [867]mainframes, partly due to pricing and the open-source
   model.^[868][120] In December 2009, computer giant [869]IBM reported
   that it would predominantly market and sell mainframe-based Enterprise
   Linux Server.^[870][121] At [871]LinuxCon North America 2015, IBM
   announced [872]LinuxONE, a series of mainframes specifically designed
   to run Linux and open-source software.^[873][122]^[874][123]

   Linux distributions are also dominant as [875]operating systems for
   [876]supercomputers.^[877][31] As of November 2017, all supercomputers
   on the [878]500 list run some variant of Linux.^[879][124]

Smart devices[[880]edit]

   Android smartphones
   [881]In-car entertainment system of the [882]Tesla Model S is based on
   [883]Ubuntu^[884][125]^[885][126]

   Several operating systems for [886]smart devices, such as
   [887]smartphones, [888]tablet computers, [889]home automation (like
   [890]Google Nest),^[891][32] [892]smart TVs ([893]Samsung and [894]LG
   [895]Smart TVs use [896]Tizen and [897]WebOS, respectively),^[898][33]
   and [899]in-vehicle infotainment (IVI) systems^[900][36] (for example
   [901]Automotive Grade Linux), are based on Linux. Major platforms for
   such systems include [902]Android, [903]Firefox OS, [904]Mer and
   [905]Tizen.

   Android has become the dominant mobile operating system for
   [906]smartphones, running on 79.3% of units sold worldwide during the
   second quarter of 2013.^[907][127] Android is also a popular operating
   system for tablets, and Android smart TVs and in-vehicle navigation
   systems have also appeared in the market.

   Although Android is based on a modified version of the Linux kernel,
   commentators disagree on whether the term "Linux distribution" applies
   to it, and whether it is "Linux" according to the common usage of the
   term. Android is a [908]Linux distribution according to the [909]Linux
   Foundation,^[910][128] Google's open-source chief [911]Chris
   DiBona,^[912][129] and several journalists.^[913][130]^[914][131]
   Others, such as Google engineer Patrick Brady, say that Android is not
   Linux in the traditional [915]Unix-like Linux distribution sense;
   Android does not include the [916]GNU C Library (it uses [917]Bionic as
   an alternative C library) and some of other components typically found
   in Linux distributions.^[918][132] [919]Ars Technica wrote that
   "Although Android is built on top of the Linux kernel, the platform has
   very little in common with the conventional desktop Linux
   stack".^[920][132]

   Cellphones and PDAs running Linux on open-source platforms became more
   common from 2007; examples include the [921]Nokia N810, [922]Openmoko's
   [923]Neo1973, and the [924]Motorola ROKR E8. Continuing the trend,
   [925]Palm (later acquired by [926]HP) produced a new Linux-derived
   operating system, [927]webOS, which is built into its line of [928]Palm
   Pre smartphones.

   [929]Nokia's [930]Maemo, one of the earliest mobile operating systems,
   was based on [931]Debian.^[932][133] It was later merged with
   [933]Intel's [934]Moblin, another Linux-based operating system, to form
   [935]MeeGo.^[936][134] The project was later terminated in favor of
   Tizen, an operating system targeted at mobile devices as well as IVI.
   Tizen is a project within [937]The Linux Foundation. Several
   [938]Samsung products are already running Tizen, [939]Samsung Gear 2
   being the most significant example.^[940][135] [941]Samsung Z
   smartphones will use Tizen instead of Android.^[942][136]

   As a result of MeeGo's termination, the Mer project forked the MeeGo
   codebase to create a basis for mobile-oriented operating
   systems.^[943][137] In July 2012, [944]Jolla announced [945]Sailfish
   OS, their own mobile operating system built upon Mer technology.
   The [946]PinePhone running [947]Plasma Mobile on [948]PostmarketOS.

   [949]Mozilla's Firefox OS consists of the Linux kernel, a [950]hardware
   abstraction layer, a [951]web-standards-based [952]runtime environment
   and user interface, and an integrated [953]web browser.^[954][138]

   [955]Canonical has released [956]Ubuntu Touch, aiming to bring
   convergence to the user experience on this mobile operating system and
   its desktop counterpart, [957]Ubuntu. The operating system also
   provides a full Ubuntu desktop when connected to an external
   monitor.^[958][139]

   The [959]Librem 5 is a smartphone developed by [960]Purism. By default,
   it runs the company-made Linux-based [961]PureOS, but it can also run
   other Linux distributions.^[962][140] Like Ubuntu Touch, PureOS is
   designed with convergence in mind, allowing desktop programs to run on
   the smartphone. An example of this is the desktop version of
   [963]Mozilla Firefox.^[964][141]

   Another smartphone is the [965]PinePhone, made by the computer
   manufacturer [966]Pine64. The PinePhone can run a variety of
   Linux-based operating systems such as Ubuntu Touch and
   [967]PostmarketOS.^[968][142]

Embedded devices[[969]edit]

   See also: [970]Embedded Linux and [971]Linux devices
   A ubiquitous [972]router running on the linux kernel.

   Due to its low cost and ease of customization, [973]Linux is often used
   in [974]embedded systems. In the non-mobile telecommunications
   equipment sector, the majority of [975]customer-premises equipment
   (CPE) hardware runs some Linux-based operating system. [976]OpenWrt is
   a community-driven example upon which many of the OEM firmware releases
   are based.

   For example, the popular [977]TiVo digital video recorder also uses a
   customized Linux,^[978][143] as do several network [979]firewalls and
   [980]routers from such makers as [981]Cisco/[982]Linksys. The [983]Korg
   OASYS, the [984]Korg KRONOS, the [985]Yamaha Motif XS/Motif XF
   [986]music workstations,^[987][144] Yamaha S90XS/S70XS, Yamaha
   MOX6/MOX8 synthesizers, Yamaha Motif-Rack XS [988]tone generator
   module, and Roland RD-700GX [989]digital piano also run Linux. Linux is
   also used in [990]stage lighting control systems, such as the
   WholeHogIII console.^[991][145]

Gaming[[992]edit]

   Main article: [993]Linux gaming

   In the past, there were few games available for Linux. In recent years,
   more games have been released with support for Linux (especially
   [994]Indie games), with the exception of a few [995]AAA title games.
   [996]Android, a popular mobile platform which uses the [997]Linux
   kernel, has gained much developer interest and is one of the main
   platforms for mobile game development along with [998]iOS operating
   system by [999]Apple for [1000]iPhone and [1001]iPad devices.

   On February 14, 2013, [1002]Valve released a Linux version of
   [1003]Steam, a popular game distribution platform on PC.^[1004][146]
   Many Steam games were ported to Linux.^[1005][147] On December 13,
   2013, Valve released [1006]SteamOS, a gaming-oriented OS based on
   Debian, for [1007]beta testing, and has plans to ship [1008]Steam
   Machines as a gaming and entertainment platform.^[1009][148] Valve has
   also developed [1010]VOGL, an [1011]OpenGL debugger intended to aid
   video game development,^[1012][149] as well as porting its [1013]Source
   game engine to desktop Linux.^[1014][150] As a result of Valve's
   effort, several prominent games such as [1015]DotA 2, [1016]Team
   Fortress 2, [1017]Portal, [1018]Portal 2 and [1019]Left 4 Dead 2 are
   now natively available on desktop Linux.

   On July 31, 2013, [1020]Nvidia released [1021]Shield as an attempt to
   use Android as a specialized gaming platform.^[1022][151]

   Some Linux users play Windows-based games using [1023]Wine or
   [1024]CrossOver Linux.

   On August 22, 2018, Valve released their own fork of Wine called
   [1025]Proton, aimed at gaming. It features some improvements over the
   vanilla Wine such as Vulkan-based DirectX 11 and 12 implementations,
   Steam integration, better full screen and game controller support and
   improved performance for multi-threaded games.^[1026][152]

Specialized uses[[1027]edit]

   Due to the flexibility, customizability and free and open-source nature
   of Linux, it becomes possible to highly tailor Linux towards a specific
   purpose. There are two main methods to assemble a specialized Linux
   distribution: building from scratch or from a general-purpose
   distribution as a base. The distributions often used for this purpose
   include [1028]Debian, [1029]Fedora, [1030]Ubuntu (which is itself based
   on Debian), [1031]Arch Linux, [1032]Gentoo, and [1033]Slackware. In
   contrast, Linux distributions built from scratch do not have
   general-purpose bases; instead, they focus on the [1034]JeOS philosophy
   by including only necessary components and avoiding [1035]resource
   overhead caused by components considered redundant in the
   distribution's use cases.

Home theater PC[[1036]edit]

   A [1037]home theater PC (HTPC) is a PC that is mainly used as an
   entertainment system, especially a [1038]home theater system. It is
   normally connected to a television, and often an additional audio
   system.

   [1039]OpenELEC, a Linux distribution that incorporates the media center
   software [1040]Kodi, is an OS tuned specifically for an HTPC. Having
   been built from the ground up adhering to the JeOS principle, the OS is
   very lightweight and very suitable for the confined usage range of an
   HTPC.

   There are also special editions of Linux distributions that include the
   [1041]MythTV media center software, such as [1042]Mythbuntu, a special
   edition of Ubuntu.

Digital security[[1043]edit]

   [1044]Kali Linux is a Debian-based Linux distribution designed for
   [1045]digital forensics and [1046]penetration testing. It comes
   preinstalled with several software applications for penetration testing
   and identifying [1047]security exploits.^[1048][153] The Ubuntu
   derivative [1049]BackBox provides pre-installed security and network
   analysis tools for ethical hacking.
   The Arch-based [1050]BlackArch includes over 2100 tools for pentesting
   and security researching.^[1051][154]

   There are many Linux distributions created with privacy, secrecy,
   network anonymity and information security in mind, including
   [1052]Tails, [1053]Tin Hat Linux and [1054]Tinfoil Hat Linux.
   [1055]Lightweight Portable Security is a distribution based on Arch
   Linux and developed by the [1056]United States Department of Defense.
   Tor-ramdisk is a minimal distribution created solely to host the
   network anonymity software [1057]Tor.

System rescue[[1058]edit]

   Linux [1059]Live CD sessions have long been used as a tool for
   recovering data from a broken computer system and for repairing the
   system. Building upon that idea, several Linux distributions tailored
   for this purpose have emerged, most of which use [1060]GParted as a
   partition editor, with additional data recovery and system repair
   software:
     * [1061]GParted Live – a Debian-based distribution developed by the
       GParted project.
     * [1062]Parted Magic – a commercial Linux distribution.
     * [1063]SystemRescueCD – an Arch-based distribution with support for
       editing Windows [1064]registry.

In space[[1065]edit]

   [1066]SpaceX uses multiple redundant [1067]flight computers in a
   [1068]fault-tolerant design in its [1069]Falcon 9 rocket. Each Merlin
   engine is controlled by three [1070]voting computers, with two physical
   processors per computer that constantly check each other's operation.
   Linux is not inherently fault-tolerant (no operating system is, as it
   is a function of the whole system including the hardware), but the
   flight computer software makes it so for its purpose.^[1071][155] For
   flexibility, [1072]commercial off-the-shelf parts and system-wide
   "radiation-tolerant" design are used instead of [1073]radiation
   hardened parts.^[1074][155] As of July 2019^[1075][update], SpaceX has
   conducted over [1076]76 launches of the Falcon 9 since 2010, out of
   which all but one have successfully delivered their primary payloads to
   the intended [1077]orbit, and has used it to transport astronauts to
   the [1078]International Space Station. The [1079]Dragon 2 crew capsule
   also uses Linux.^[1080][38]

   Windows was deployed as the operating system on non-mission critical
   laptops used on the space station, but it was later replaced with
   Linux. [1081]Robonaut 2, the first humanoid robot in space, is also
   Linux-based.^[1082][156]

   The [1083]Jet Propulsion Laboratory has used Linux for a number of
   years "to help with projects relating to the construction of unmanned
   space flight and deep space exploration"; [1084]NASA uses Linux in
   robotics in the Mars rover, and [1085]Ubuntu Linux to "save data from
   satellites".^[1086][157]

Education[[1087]edit]

   Linux distributions have been created to provide hands-on experience
   with coding and source code to students, on devices such as the
   [1088]Raspberry Pi. In addition to producing a practical device, the
   intention is to show students "how things work under the
   hood".^[1089][158]

   The Ubuntu derivatives [1090]Edubuntu and [1091]The Linux Schools
   Project, as well as the Debian derivative [1092]Skolelinux, provide
   education-oriented software packages. They also include tools for
   administering and building school computer labs and computer-based
   classrooms, such as the [1093]Linux Terminal Server Project (LTSP).

Others[[1094]edit]

   Instant WebKiosk and [1095]Webconverger are browser-based Linux
   distributions often used in web [1096]kiosks and [1097]digital signage.
   [1098]Thinstation is a minimalist distribution designed for [1099]thin
   clients. [1100]Rocks Cluster Distribution is tailored for
   [1101]high-performance computing clusters.

   There are general-purpose Linux distributions that target a specific
   audience, such as users of a specific language or geographical area.
   Such examples include [1102]Ubuntu Kylin for Chinese language users and
   BlankOn targeted at Indonesians. Profession-specific distributions
   include [1103]Ubuntu Studio for media creation and [1104]DNALinux for
   [1105]bioinformatics. There is also a Muslim-oriented distribution of
   the name [1106]Sabily that consequently also provides some Islamic
   tools. Certain organizations use slightly specialized Linux
   distributions internally, including [1107]GendBuntu used by the French
   [1108]National Gendarmerie, [1109]Goobuntu used internally by Google,
   and [1110]Astra Linux developed specifically for the Russian army.

Market share and uptake[[1111]edit]

   Main article: [1112]Linux adoption
   See also: [1113]Usage share of operating systems

   Many quantitative studies of [1114]free/open-source software focus on
   topics including market share and reliability, with numerous studies
   specifically examining Linux.^[1115][159] The Linux market is growing
   rapidly, and the revenue of servers, desktops, and packaged software
   running Linux was expected to^[[1116]needs update] exceed $35.7 billion
   by 2008.^[1117][160] Analysts and proponents attribute the relative
   success of Linux to its security, reliability, low cost, and freedom
   from [1118]vendor lock-in.^[1119][161]^[1120][162]

   Desktops and laptops
          According to [1121]web server statistics (that is, based on the
          numbers recorded from visits to websites by client devices), as
          of November 2018^[1122][update], the estimated market share of
          Linux on [1123]desktop computers is around 2.1%. In comparison,
          [1124]Microsoft Windows has a market share of around 87%, while
          [1125]macOS covers around 9.7%.^[1126][25]

   Web servers

          W3Cook publishes stats that use the top 1,000,000 Alexa
          domains,^[1127][163] which as of May 2015^[1128][update]
          estimate that 96.55% of web servers run Linux, 1.73% run
          Windows, and 1.72% run FreeBSD.^[1129][164]

          W3Techs publishes stats that use the top 10,000,000 Alexa
          domains, updated monthly^[1130][165] and as of
          November 2016^[1131][update] estimate that 66.7% of web servers
          run Linux/Unix, and 33.4% run Microsoft Windows.^[1132][166]

          In September 2008, Microsoft's then-CEO [1133]Steve Ballmer
          stated that 60% of web servers ran Linux, versus 40% that ran
          [1134]Windows Server.^[1135][167]

          [1136]IDC's Q1 2007 report indicated that Linux held 12.7% of
          the overall server market at that time;^[1137][168] this
          estimate was based on the number of Linux servers sold by
          various companies, and did not include server hardware purchased
          separately that had Linux installed on it later.

   Mobile devices
          Android, which is based on the Linux kernel, has become the
          dominant operating system for [1138]smartphones. During the
          second quarter of 2013, 79.3% of smartphones sold worldwide used
          Android.^[1139][127] Android is also a popular operating system
          for tablets, being responsible for more than 60% of tablet sales
          as of 2013.^[1140][169] According to web server statistics, as
          of December 2014^[1141][update] Android has a market share of
          about 46%, with [1142]iOS holding 45%, and the remaining 9%
          attributed to various niche platforms.^[1143][170]

   Film production
          For years Linux has been the platform of choice in the film
          industry. The first major film produced on Linux servers was
          1997's [1144]Titanic.^[1145][171]^[1146][172] Since then major
          studios including [1147]DreamWorks Animation, [1148]Pixar,
          [1149]Weta Digital, and [1150]Industrial Light & Magic have
          migrated to Linux.^[1151][173]^[1152][174]^[1153][175] According
          to the Linux Movies Group, more than 95% of the servers and
          desktops at large animation and visual effects companies use
          Linux.^[1154][176]

   Use in government
          Linux distributions have also gained popularity with various
          local and national governments. News of the Russian military
          creating its own Linux distribution has also surfaced, and has
          come to fruition as the G.H.ost Project.^[1155][177] The Indian
          state of [1156]Kerala has gone to the extent of mandating that
          all state high schools run Linux on their
          computers.^[1157][178]^[1158][179] [1159]China uses Linux
          exclusively as the operating system for its [1160]Loongson
          processor family to achieve technology independence.^[1161][180]
          In Spain, some regions have developed their own Linux
          distributions, which are widely used in education and official
          institutions, like gnuLinEx in Extremadura and Guadalinex in
          Andalusia. [1162]France and [1163]Germany have also taken steps
          toward the adoption of Linux.^[1164][181] North Korea's
          [1165]Red Star OS, developed since 2002, is based on a version
          of [1166]Fedora Linux.^[1167][182]

Copyright, trademark, and naming[[1168]edit]

   See also: [1169]GNU/Linux naming controversy and [1170]SCO–Linux
   disputes

   Linux kernel is [1171]licensed under the [1172]GNU General Public
   License (GPL), version 2. The GPL requires that anyone who distributes
   software based on source code under this license must make the
   originating source code (and any modifications) available to the
   recipient under the same terms.^[1173][183] Other key components of a
   typical Linux distribution are also mainly licensed under the GPL, but
   they may use other licenses; many libraries use the [1174]GNU Lesser
   General Public License (LGPL), a more permissive variant of the GPL,
   and the [1175]X.Org implementation of the [1176]X Window System uses
   the [1177]MIT License.

   Torvalds states that the Linux kernel will not move from version 2 of
   the GPL to version 3.^[1178][184]^[1179][185] He specifically dislikes
   some provisions in the new license which prohibit the use of the
   software in [1180]digital rights management.^[1181][186] It would also
   be impractical to obtain permission from all the copyright holders, who
   number in the thousands.^[1182][187]

   A 2001 study of [1183]Red Hat Linux 7.1 found that this distribution
   contained 30 million [1184]source lines of code.^[1185][188] Using the
   [1186]Constructive Cost Model, the study estimated that this
   distribution required about eight thousand person-years of development
   time. According to the study, if all this software had been developed
   by conventional [1187]proprietary means, it would have cost about
   $1.6 billion (2021 US dollars) to develop in the United
   States.^[1188][188] Most of the source code (71%) was written in the
   [1189]C programming language, but many other languages were used,
   including [1190]C++, [1191]Lisp, [1192]assembly language, [1193]Perl,
   [1194]Python, [1195]Fortran, and various [1196]shell scripting
   languages. Slightly over half of all lines of code were licensed under
   the GPL. The Linux kernel itself was 2.4 million lines of code, or 8%
   of the total.^[1197][188]

   In a later study, the same analysis was performed for [1198]Debian
   version 4.0 (etch, which was released in 2007).^[1199][189] This
   distribution contained close to 283 million source lines of code, and
   the study estimated that it would have required about seventy three
   thousand man-years and cost US$8.84 billion (in 2021 dollars) to
   develop by conventional means.

   The name "Linux" is also used for a laundry detergent made by Swiss
   company Rösch.^[1200][190]

   In the United States, the name Linux is a trademark registered to Linus
   Torvalds.^[1201][8] Initially, nobody registered it, but on August 15,
   1994, William R. Della Croce, Jr. filed for the trademark Linux, and
   then demanded royalties from Linux distributors. In 1996, Torvalds and
   some affected organizations sued him to have the trademark assigned to
   Torvalds, and, in 1997, the case was settled.^[1202][191] The licensing
   of the trademark has since been handled by the [1203]Linux Mark
   Institute (LMI). Torvalds has stated that he trademarked the name only
   to prevent someone else from using it. LMI originally charged a nominal
   sublicensing fee for use of the Linux name as part of
   trademarks,^[1204][192] but later changed this in favor of offering a
   free, perpetual worldwide sublicense.^[1205][193]

   The [1206]Free Software Foundation (FSF) prefers GNU/Linux as the name
   when referring to the operating system as a whole, because it considers
   Linux distributions to be [1207]variants of the [1208]GNU operating
   system initiated in 1983 by [1209]Richard Stallman, president of the
   FSF.^[1210][14]^[1211][15] They explicitly take no issue over the name
   Android for the Android OS, which is also an operating system based on
   the Linux kernel, as GNU is not a part of it.

   A minority of public figures and software projects other than Stallman
   and the FSF, notably [1212]Debian (which had been sponsored by the FSF
   up to 1996),^[1213][194] also use GNU/Linux when referring to the
   operating system as a whole.^[1214][143]^[1215][195]^[1216][196] Most
   media and common usage, however, refers to this family of operating
   systems simply as Linux, as do many large Linux distributions (for
   example, [1217]SUSE Linux and [1218]Red Hat Enterprise Linux). By
   contrast, Linux distributions containing only free software use
   "GNU/Linux" or simply "GNU", such as [1219]Trisquel GNU/Linux,
   [1220]Parabola GNU/Linux-libre, [1221]BLAG Linux and GNU, and
   [1222]gNewSense.

   As of May 2011^[1223][update], about 8% to 13% of a modern Linux
   distribution is made of GNU components (the range depending on whether
   [1224]GNOME is considered part of GNU), as determined by counting
   [1225]lines of source code making up Ubuntu's "Natty" release;
   meanwhile, 6% is taken by the Linux kernel, increased to 9% when
   including its direct dependencies.^[1226][197]

Notes[[1243]edit]

    1. [1244]^ GNU is the primary userland used in nearly all Linux
       distributions.^[1245][2]^[1246][3]^[1247][4] The GNU userland
       contains system daemons, user applications, the GUI, and various
       libraries. [1248]GNU Core utilities are an essential part of most
       distributions. Most Linux distributions use the [1249]X Window
       system.^[1250][5] Other components of the userland, such as the
       [1251]widget toolkit, vary with the specific distribution, desktop
       environment, and user configuration.^[1252][6]
    2. [1253]^ "Linux" trademark is owned by [1254]Linus
       Torvalds^[1255][8] and administered by the [1256]Linux Mark
       Institute.

   193. [1856]^ [1857]"Linux Mark Institute". [1858]Archived from the
       original on February 13, 2008. Retrieved February 24, 2008. "LMI
       has restructured its sublicensing program. Our new sublicense
       agreement is: Free – approved sublicense holders pay no fees;
       Perpetual – sublicense terminates only in breach of the agreement
       or when your organization ceases to use its mark; Worldwide – one
       sublicense covers your use of the mark anywhere in the world"
   194. [1859]^ Richard Stallman (April 28, 1996). [1860]"The FSF is no
       longer sponsoring Debian". tech-insider.org. [1861]Archived from
       the original on February 21, 2014. Retrieved February 8, 2014.
   195. [1862]^ [1863]"About Debian". debian.org. December 8, 2013.
       [1864]Archived from the original on January 23, 2014. Retrieved
       January 30, 2014.
   196. [1865]^ Andrew D. Balsa; [1866]Coauthors. [1867]"The linux-kernel
       mailing list FAQ". The Linux Kernel Archives. Kernel.org.
       [1868]Archived from the original on October 1, 2012. Retrieved June
       13, 2013. "...we have tried to use the word "Linux" or the
       expression "Linux kernel" to designate the kernel, and GNU/Linux to
       designate the entire body of GNU/GPL'ed OS software,... ...many
       people forget that the linux kernel mailing list is a forum for
       discussion of kernel-related matters, not GNU/Linux in general..."
   197. [1869]^ Côrte-Real, Pedro (May 31, 2011). [1870]"How much GNU is
       there in GNU/Linux?". Split Perspective. [1871]Archived from the
       original on February 7, 2014. Retrieved January 28, 2014.
       ([1872]self-published data)
